rel="nofollow">data</a></td></tr><tr style="vertical-align: top;"><td><b>ARICNS</b></td><td><a class="external text" href="http://wwwadd.zah.uni-heidelberg.de/datenbanken/aricns/cnspages/4c01151.htm" rel="nofollow">data</a></td></tr><tr><td><b><a href="/wiki/Extrasolar_Planets_Encyclopaedia" title="Extrasolar Planets Encyclopaedia">Extrasolar Planets<br/>Encyclopaedia</a></b></td><td><a class="external text" href="http://exoplanet.eu/star.php?st=alf+cen" rel="nofollow">data</a></td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p><b>Alpha Centauri</b> (<a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">Latinized</a> from <b>&#945; Centauri</b>, abbreviated <b>Alpha Cen</b> or <b>&#945; Cen</b>) is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars_and_brown_dwarfs" title="List of nearest stars and brown dwarfs">closest</a> <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star system</a> and closest <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary system</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> at 4.37 light-years (1.34&#160;pc) from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. It is a triple star system, consisting of three <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>: &#945; Centauri A (officially <b>Rigil Kentaurus</b>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-LSN_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-LSN-16">[15]</a></sup> &#945; Centauri B (officially <b>Toliman</b>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-LSN_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-LSN-16">[15]</a></sup> and &#945; Centauri C (officially <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-LSN_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-LSN-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Alpha Centauri A and B are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun-like" title="Sun-like">Sun-like</a> stars (<a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">Class G</a> and <a href="/wiki/K-type_main-sequence_star" title="K-type main-sequence star">K</a>), and together they form the <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> Alpha Centauri AB. To the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>, the two main components appear to be a single star with an <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of &#8722;0.27, forming the brightest star in the southern <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaurus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_brightest_stars" title="List of brightest stars">third-brightest</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a>, outshone only by <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>.\n</p><p>Alpha Centauri A has 1.1 times the mass and 1.519 times the <a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity">luminosity of the Sun</a>, while Alpha Centauri B is smaller and cooler, at 0.907 times the <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">Sun\'s mass</a> and 0.445 times its luminosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The pair orbit about a <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">common centre</a> with an orbital period of 79.91 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-18">[17]</a></sup> Their elliptical orbit is <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a>, so that the distance between A and B varies from 35.6 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (AU), to that between <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> and the Sun (11.2 AU).\n</p><p>Alpha Centauri C, or Proxima Centauri, is a small and faint <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a> (<a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification#Class_M" title="Stellar classification">Class M</a>). Though not visible to the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>, Proxima Centauri is the closest star to the Sun at a distance of 4.24 light-years (1.30&#160;pc), slightly closer than Alpha Centauri AB. Currently, the distance between Proxima Centauri and Alpha Centauri AB is about 13,000 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (0.21&#160;ly),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kervella2017_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kervella2017-19">[18]</a></sup> equivalent to about 430 times the radius of <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>\'s orbit. <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">Proxima Centauri b</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">Earth-sized</a> <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanet</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a> of Proxima Centauri; it was discovered in 2016.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" href="#Hypothetical_planets_or_exploration"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Hypothetical planets or exploration</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><i>&#945; Centauri</i> (<a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">Latinized</a> to <i>Alpha Centauri</i>) is the system\'s <a href="/wiki/Bayer_designation" title="Bayer designation">Bayer designation</a>. It once bore the name <i>Rigil Kentaurus</i>, which is a Latinisation of the Arabic name <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" title="Arabic language text">&#1585;&#1580;&#1604; &#1575;&#1604;&#1602;&#1606;&#1591;&#1608;&#1585;&#1587;</span>&#8206; <i>Rijl al-Qan&#7789;&#363;ris,</i> meaning \'Foot of the <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaur</a>\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K&amp;S_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-K&amp;S-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Alpha Centauri C</i> was discovered in 1915 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._A._Innes" title="Robert T. A. Innes">Robert T. A. Innes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Innes1915_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Innes1915-22">[21]</a></sup> who suggested that it be named <i>Proxima Centaurus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Innes1917_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Innes1917-23">[22]</a></sup> later amended to <i>Proxima Centauri</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aj39_913_20_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-aj39_913_20-24">[23]</a></sup> The name is from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, meaning \'nearest [star] of <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaurus</a>\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford2010_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford2010-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/IAU_Working_Group_on_Star_Names" title="IAU Working Group on Star Names">Working Group on Star Names</a> of the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WGSN_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-WGSN-26">[25]</a></sup> having decided to attribute proper names to individual stars rather than entire <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiple_star" title="Multiple star">multiple systems</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TriRpt18_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-TriRpt18-27">[26]</a></sup> approved the names <i>Rigil Kentaurus</i> for <i>Alpha Centauri A</i> and <i>Proxima Centauri</i> for <i>Alpha Centauri C</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-LSN_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-LSN-16">[15]</a></sup> In 10 August 2018, IAU approved the name <i>Toliman</i> for <i>Alpha Centauri B</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-CSN_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-CSN-28">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stellar_system">Stellar system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Stellar system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Alpha Centauri is a triple star system, with its two main stars, Alpha Centauri&#160;A and Alpha Centauri&#160;B, being a <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary</a> component. The <i>AB</i> designation, or older <i>A&#215;B</i>, denotes the mass centre of a main binary system relative to companion star(s) in a multiple star system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoubleStarsHeintz_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleStarsHeintz-29">[28]</a></sup> <i>AB-C</i> refers to the component of Proxima Centauri in relation to the central binary, being the distance between the centre of mass and the outlying companion. Because the distance between Proxima (C) and either of Alpha Centauri&#160;A or B is similar, the AB binary system is sometimes treated as a single gravitational object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wds1996_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-wds1996-30">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_properties">Orbital properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Orbital properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png/330px-Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png/495px-Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png/660px-Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orbit_Alpha_Centauri_AB_arcsec.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apparent and true orbits of Alpha Centauri. The A component is held stationary, and the relative orbital motion of the B component is shown. The apparent orbit (thin ellipse) is the shape of the orbit as seen by an observer on Earth. The true orbit is the shape of the orbit viewed perpendicular to the plane of the orbital motion. According to the radial velocity vs. time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pourbaix_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pourbaix-15">[14]</a></sup> the radial separation of A and B along the line of sight had reached a maximum in 2007, with B being further from Earth than A. The orbit is divided here into 80 points: each step refers to a timestep of approx. 0.99888 years or 364.84 days.</div></div></div>\n<p>The A and B components of Alpha Centauri have an orbital period of 79.91&#160;years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-18">[17]</a></sup> Their orbit is moderately <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a>, <i>e</i>&#160;=&#160;0.5179;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-18">[17]</a></sup> their closest approach is 11.2&#160;AU (1.68&#160;billion&#160;km), or about the distance between the Sun and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>; and their furthest separation is 35.6&#160;AU (5.33&#160;billion&#160;km), about the distance between the Sun and <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-18">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Viewed from Earth, the <i>apparent orbit</i> of A and B means that their separation and <a href="/wiki/Position_angle" title="Position angle">position angle</a> (PA) are in continuous change throughout their projected orbit. The closest recent approach was in February 2016, at 4.0&#160;arcsec through the PA of 300&#176;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> The observed maximum separation of these stars is about 22&#160;arcsec, while the minimum distance is 1.7&#160;arcsec.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken-32">[31]</a></sup> The widest separation occurred during February 1976, and the next will be in January 2056.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-18">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most recent, <i>true orbit</i>, closest approach or <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">periastron</a> was in August 1955, and the next will be in May 2035. The furthest orbital separation or <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">apastron</a> last occurred in May 1995, and the next will be in 2075. The apparent distance between Alpha Centauri A and B is rapidly decreasing, at least until 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-18">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Alpha Centauri C is about 13,000 astronomical units (AU) away from Alpha Centauri AB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kervella2017_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kervella2017-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adsabs.harvard.edu_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-adsabs.harvard.edu-34">[33]</a></sup> This is equivalent to 0.21&#160;ly or 1.9&#160;trillion&#160;km&#8212;about 5% the distance between Alpha Centauri AB and the Sun. For a long time, estimates of Proxima\'s small orbital speed around AB were insufficiently accurate to determine whether Proxima Centauri is bound to the Alpha Centauri system or an unrelated star that happens to be passing by at a low speed.\n</p><p>Radial velocity measurements made in 2017 were precise enough to show that Proxima Centauri and Alpha Centauri AB are gravitationally bound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kervella2017_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kervella2017-19">[18]</a></sup> The orbital period of Proxima Centauri is approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005547000000000000&#9824;</span>547<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+6600<br/>&#8722;4000</span></span></span> years, with an eccentricity of 0.50 &#177; 0.08, more eccentric than <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury\'s</a>. Proxima Centauri comes within <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7014643270844010000&#9824;</span>4300<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+1100<br/>&#8722;900</span></span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a></span> of AB at periastron, and the apastron occurs at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7015194477231910000&#9824;</span>13<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+300<br/>&#8722;100</span></span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kervella2017_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kervella2017-19">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="164" data-file-width="452" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg/280px-Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg/420px-Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg/560px-Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Centauri_relative_sizes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The relative sizes and colours of stars in the Alpha Centauri system, compared to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">Asteroseismic</a> studies, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromospheric_activity" title="Chromospheric activity">chromospheric activity</a>, and stellar rotation (<a href="/wiki/Gyrochronology" title="Gyrochronology">gyrochronology</a>) are all consistent with the Alpha Centauri system being similar in age to, or slightly older than, the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mam08_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mam08-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Asteroseismic analyses that incorporate tight observational constraints on the stellar parameters for the Alpha Centauri stars have yielded age estimates of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000484999999999999&#9824;</span>4.85<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.5</span>&#160;Gyr,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thevenin02_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-Thevenin02-8">[7]</a></sup> <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000500000000000000&#9824;</span>5.0<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.5</span>&#160;Gyr,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bazot12_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bazot12-36">[35]</a></sup> 5.2 &#177; 1.9&#160;Gyr,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miglio05_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miglio05-37">[36]</a></sup> 6.4&#160;Gyr,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thoul03_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thoul03-38">[37]</a></sup> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000652000000000000&#9824;</span>6.52<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.3</span>&#160;Gyr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggenberger04_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eggenberger04-39">[38]</a></sup> Age estimates for the stars based on chromospheric activity (Calcium H &amp; K emission) yield 4.4 &#177; 2.1 Gyr,&#160;whereas gyrochronology yields <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000500000000000000&#9824;</span>5.0<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.3</span>&#160;Gyr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mam08_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mam08-35">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">Stellar evolution</a> theory implies both stars are slightly older than the Sun at 5 to 6&#160;billion years, as derived by their mass and spectral characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stellar-model-kim_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-stellar-model-kim-40">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbital elements</a>, the total mass of Alpha Centauri AB is about 2.0&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[note 2]</a></sup>&#8212;or twice that of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken-32">[31]</a></sup> The average individual stellar masses are 1.09&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> and 0.90&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>, respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stellar-model-kim_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-stellar-model-kim-40">[39]</a></sup> though slightly higher masses have been quoted in recent years, such as 1.14&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> and 0.92&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RECONS_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-RECONS-42">[40]</a></sup> or totalling 2.06&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>. Alpha Centauri A and B have <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitudes</a> of +4.38 and +5.71, respectively. \n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_A">Alpha Centauri A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Alpha Centauri A, also known as Rigil Kentaurus, is the principal member, or <i>primary</i>, of the binary system. It is a solar-like <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> star with a similar yellowish colour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csiro_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-csiro-43">[41]</a></sup> whose <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">stellar classification</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_type" title="Spectral type">spectral type</a> G2&#160;V;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-torres2006_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-torres2006-4">[3]</a></sup> it is slightly larger and more luminous than the Sun. Alpha Centauri A is about 10 percent more massive than the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thevenin02_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-Thevenin02-8">[7]</a></sup> with a radius about 22 percent larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kervella2017_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-kervella2017-9">[8]</a></sup> When considered among the individual <a href="/wiki/List_of_brightest_stars" title="List of brightest stars">brightest stars</a> in the sky (excluding the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>), it is the fourth brightest at an apparent magnitude of &#8722;0.01, being slightly fainter than <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">Arcturus</a> at an <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of &#8722;0.04.\n</p><p>The type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_activity" title="Magnetic activity">magnetic activity</a> on Alpha Centauri A is comparable to that of the Sun, showing <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">coronal</a> variability due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_spot" title="Star spot">star spots</a>, as modulated by the rotation of the star. However, since 2005 the activity level has fallen into a deep minimum that might be similar to the Sun\'s historical <a href="/wiki/Maunder_Minimum" title="Maunder Minimum">Maunder Minimum</a>. Alternatively, it may have a very long stellar activity cycle and is slowly recovering from a minimum phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ayres2014_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ayres2014-44">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_B">Alpha Centauri B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Alpha Centauri B, also known as Toliman, is the <i>secondary</i> star of the binary system. It is a main-sequence star of spectral type K1&#160;V, making it more an orange colour than Alpha Centauri A;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csiro_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-csiro-43">[41]</a></sup> it has around 90 percent the mass of the Sun and a 14 percent smaller diameter. Although it has a lower luminosity than A, Alpha Centauri B emits more energy in the <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xrays_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xrays-45">[43]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> varies on a short time scale, and there has been at least one observed <a href="/wiki/Flare_star" title="Flare star">flare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xrays_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Xrays-45">[43]</a></sup> It is more magnetically active than Alpha Centauri A, showing a cycle of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7008258772319999999&#9824;</span>8.2<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.2&#160;yr</span> compared to 11 years for the Sun, and about half the minimum-to-peak variation in coronal luminosity of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ayres2014_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ayres2014-44">[42]</a></sup> Alpha Centauri B has an apparent magnitude of +1.35, slightly dimmer than <a href="/wiki/Beta_Crucis" title="Beta Crucis">Beta Crucis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-LSN_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-LSN-16">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_C">Alpha Centauri C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a></div>\n<p>Alpha Centauri C, better known as Proxima Centauri, is a small main-sequence <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a> of spectral class M6&#160;Ve. It has an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of +15.60, over 20,000 times fainter than the Sun. Its mass is calculated to be <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999122100000000000&#9824;</span>0.1221</span>&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KervellaTh&#233;venin2017_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-KervellaTh&#233;venin2017-46">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="overflow:auto;"><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:1002px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relative_positions_of_Sun,_Alpha_Centauri_AB_and_Proxima_Centauri.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Relative_positions_of_Sun%2C_Alpha_Centauri_AB_and_Proxima_Centauri.png" width="1000"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relative_positions_of_Sun,_Alpha_Centauri_AB_and_Proxima_Centauri.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relative positions of Sun, Alpha Centauri AB and Proxima Centauri. Grey dot is projection of Proxima Centauri, located at the same distance as Alpha Centauri AB.</div></div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Observation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="noviewer thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px"><div style="position:relative;width:280px;border:1px solid lightgray"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Position_Alpha_Cen.png" title="Alpha Centauri is located in 100x100"><img alt="Alpha Centauri is located in 100x100" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="710" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Position_Alpha_Cen.png/280px-Position_Alpha_Cen.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Position_Alpha_Cen.png/420px-Position_Alpha_Cen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Position_Alpha_Cen.png/560px-Position_Alpha_Cen.png 2x" width="280"/></a><div style="position:absolute;top:83%;left:24%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-10px;top:-10px;line-height:0"><img alt="Alpha Centauri" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cercle_rouge_100%25.svg/20px-Cercle_rouge_100%25.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cercle_rouge_100%25.svg/30px-Cercle_rouge_100%25.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cercle_rouge_100%25.svg/40px-Cercle_rouge_100%25.svg.png 2x" title="Alpha Centauri" width="20"/></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Position_Alpha_Cen.png" title="File:Position Alpha Cen.png"> </a></div>Location of Alpha Centauri in Centaurus</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="853" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg/220px-Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg/330px-Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg/440px-Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Our_Nearest_Star_System_Observed_Live.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The two bright stars at the lower right are Alpha (right) and <a href="/wiki/Beta_Centauri" title="Beta Centauri">Beta Centauri</a> (left, above antenna). A line drawn through them points to the four bright stars of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Cross" title="Southern Cross">Southern Cross</a>, just to the right of the dome of <a href="/wiki/La_Silla_Observatory" title="La Silla Observatory">La Silla Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>To the naked eye, Alpha Centauri AB appears to be a single star, the <a href="/wiki/First_magnitude_star" title="First magnitude star">brightest</a> in the southern constellation of <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaurus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_encyclopedia_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_encyclopedia-48">[46]</a></sup> Their apparent angular separation varies over about 80 years between 2 and 22&#160;arcsec (the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a> has a resolution of 60&#160;arcsec),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> but through much of the orbit, both are easily resolved in binoculars or small telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2a_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2a-50">[48]</a></sup> At &#8722;0.27 <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> (<a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude#Magnitude_addition" title="Apparent magnitude">combined</a> for A and B magnitudes), Alpha Centauri is fainter only than <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_encyclopedia_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_encyclopedia-48">[46]</a></sup> It forms the outer star of <i>The Pointers</i> or <i>The Southern Pointers</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2a_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2a-50">[48]</a></sup> so called because the line through <a href="/wiki/Beta_Centauri" title="Beta Centauri">Beta Centauri</a> (Hadar/Agena),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NortonSA_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-NortonSA-51">[49]</a></sup>\nsome 4.5<a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">&#176;</a> west,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2a_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2a-50">[48]</a></sup> points to the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Crux" title="Crux">Crux</a>&#8212;the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Cross" title="Southern Cross">Southern Cross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2a_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2a-50">[48]</a></sup> The Pointers easily distinguish the true Southern Cross from the fainter <a href="/wiki/Asterism_(astronomy)" title="Asterism (astronomy)">asterism</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Vela_(constellation)" title="Vela (constellation)">False Cross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alpha-Centauri-daylight.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Alpha-Centauri-daylight.png/220px-Alpha-Centauri-daylight.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/Alpha-Centauri-daylight.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alpha-Centauri-daylight.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alpha Centauri AB taken in daylight by holding a Canon Powershot S100 behind the eyepiece of a 110&#160;mm refractor. The photo is one of the best frames of a video. The <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">double star</a> is clearly visible.</div></div></div>\n<p>South of about 29&#176; S latitude, Alpha Centauri is <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_star" title="Circumpolar star">circumpolar</a> and never sets below the horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[note 3]</a></sup> North of about 29&#176; N latitude, Alpha Centauri never rises. Alpha Centauri lies close to the southern horizon when viewed from the <a href="/wiki/29th_parallel_north" title="29th parallel north">29&#176; N latitude</a> to the equator (close to <a href="/wiki/Hermosillo" title="Hermosillo">Hermosillo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_City" title="Chihuahua City">Chihuahua City</a> in Mexico, <a href="/wiki/Galveston,_Texas" title="Galveston, Texas">Galveston, Texas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocala,_Florida" title="Ocala, Florida">Ocala, Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lanzarote" title="Lanzarote">Lanzarote</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> of Spain), but only for a short time around its <a href="/wiki/Culmination" title="Culmination">culmination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NortonSA_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-NortonSA-51">[49]</a></sup> The star culminates each year at local midnight on 24 April and at local 9 p.m. on 8 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NortonSA_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-NortonSA-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As seen from Earth, Proxima Centauri is 2.2&#176; southwest from Alpha Centauri AB, about four times the <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular diameter</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-33">[32]</a></sup>  Proxima Centauri appears as a deep-red star of a typical apparent magnitude of 11.1 in a sparsely populated star field, requiring moderately sized telescopes to be seen. Listed as V645 Cen in the <i><a href="/wiki/General_Catalogue_of_Variable_Stars" title="General Catalogue of Variable Stars">General Catalogue of Variable Stars</a> Version 4.2</i>, this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_Ceti" title="UV Ceti">UV Ceti</a>-type <a href="/wiki/Flare_star" title="Flare star">flare star</a> can unexpectedly brighten rapidly by as much as 0.6 magnitudes at visual wavelengths, then fade after only a few minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bendict1998_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-bendict1998-55">[52]</a></sup> Some amateur and professional astronomers regularly monitor for outbursts using either optical or radio telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> In August 2015, the largest recorded flares of the star occurred, with the star becoming 8.3 times brighter than normal on 13 August, in the <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude#Standard_reference_values" title="Apparent magnitude">B band (blue light region)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proximaflare_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-proximaflare-57">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_history">Observational history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Observational history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="10695" data-file-width="10675" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg/220px-The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg/330px-The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg/440px-The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_bright_star_Alpha_Centauri_and_its_surroundings.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of Alpha Centauri from the <a href="/wiki/Digitized_Sky_Survey" title="Digitized Sky Survey">Digitized Sky Survey</a>&#160;2</div></div></div><p>\nAlpha Centauri is listed in the 2nd-century <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">star catalog of Ptolemy</a>. He gave its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecliptic_coordinates" title="Ecliptic coordinates">ecliptic coordinates</a>, but texts differ as to whether the ecliptic latitude reads <span class="nowrap">44&#176; 10&#8242; South</span> or <span class="nowrap">41&#176; 10&#8242; South</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> (Presently the ecliptic latitude is <span class="nowrap">43.5&#176; South</span>, but it has decreased by a fraction of a degree since Ptolemy\'s time due to <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motion</a>.) In Ptolemy\'s time, Alpha Centauri was visible from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Egypt" title="Alexandria, Egypt">Alexandria, Egypt</a>, at <span class="nowrap">31&#176; N,</span> but, due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precession_of_the_equinoxes" title="Precession of the equinoxes">precession</a>, its declination is now <span class="nowrap">&#8211;60&#176; 51&#8242; South</span>, and it can no longer be seen at that latitude. English explorer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hues" title="Robert Hues">Robert Hues</a> brought Alpha Centauri to the attention of European observers in his 1592 work <i>Tractatus de Globis</i>, along with <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Achernar" title="Achernar">Achernar</a>, noting:</p><blockquote><p>"Now, therefore, there are but three <a href="/wiki/First_magnitude_star" title="First magnitude star">Stars of the first magnitude</a> that I could perceive in all those parts which are never seene here in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. The first of these is that bright Star in the sterne of <a href="/wiki/Argo_Navis" title="Argo Navis">Argo</a> which they call <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canobus</a>. The second [<a href="/wiki/Achernar" title="Achernar">Achernar</a>] is in the end of <a href="/wiki/Eridanus_(constellation)" title="Eridanus (constellation)">Eridanus</a>. The third [Alpha Centauri] is in the right foote of the <a href="/wiki/Centaurus" title="Centaurus">Centaure</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knobel_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-knobel-59">[56]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>The <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary</a> nature of Alpha Centauri AB was recognised in December 1689 by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Richaud&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jean Richaud (page does not exist)">Jean Richaud</a>, while observing a passing <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> from his station in <a href="/wiki/Puducherry" title="Puducherry">Puducherry</a>. Alpha Centauri was only the second binary star to be discovered, preceded by <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Crucis" title="Alpha Crucis">Alpha Crucis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KameswaraRao1984_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-KameswaraRao1984-60">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The large proper motion of Alpha Centauri AB was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Manuel_John_Johnson" title="Manuel John Johnson">Manuel John Johnson</a>, observing from <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a>, who informed <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henderson_(astronomer)" title="Thomas Henderson (astronomer)">Thomas Henderson</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Royal Observatory, Cape of Good Hope">Royal Observatory, Cape of Good Hope</a> of it. The parallax of Alpha Centauri was subsequently determined by Henderson from many exacting positional observations of the AB system between April 1832 and May 1833. He withheld his results, however, because he suspected they were too large to be true, but eventually published them in 1839 after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Bessel" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel">Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel</a> released his own accurately determined parallax for <a href="/wiki/61_Cygni" title="61 Cygni">61 Cygni</a> in 1838.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated345_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated345-61">[58]</a></sup> For this reason, Alpha Centauri is sometimes considered as the second star to have its distance measured because Henderson\'s work was not fully acknowledged at first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated345_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated345-61">[58]</a></sup> (The distance of Alpha Centauri from the Earth is now reckoned at 4.396&#160;<a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">ly</a> or 41.59&#160;trillion&#160;km.)\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="670" data-file-width="1026" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg/220px-Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg/330px-Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg/440px-Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Best_image_of_Alpha_Centauri_A_and_B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Compared to the Sun, Alpha Centauri A is of the same stellar type G2, while Alpha Centauri B is a K1-type star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Later, <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> made the first <a href="/wiki/Micrometer" title="Micrometer">micrometrical</a> observations in 1834.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JHerschel1847_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-JHerschel1847-63">[60]</a></sup> Since the early 20th century, measures have been made with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photographic_plates" title="Photographic plates">photographic plates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adsabs.harvard.edu_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-adsabs.harvard.edu-34">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1926, <a href="/wiki/William_Stephen_Finsen" title="William Stephen Finsen">William Stephen Finsen</a> calculated the approximate <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbit elements</a> close to those now accepted for this system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken-32">[31]</a></sup> All future positions are now sufficiently accurate for visual observers to determine the relative places of the stars from a binary star <a href="/wiki/Ephemeris" title="Ephemeris">ephemeris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> Others, like D. Pourbaix (2002), have regularly refined the precision of new published orbital elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SixthCatOrbVisBin_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-SixthCatOrbVisBin-18">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Robert_T._A._Innes" title="Robert T. A. Innes">Robert T. A. Innes</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a> in 1915 by blinking photographic plates taken at different times during a <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motion</a> survey. These showed large proper motion and parallax similar in both size and direction to those of Alpha Centauri AB, suggesting that Proxima Centauri is part of the Alpha Centauri system and slightly closer to Earth than Alpha Centauri AB. Lying 4.24&#160;ly (1.30&#160;pc) away, Proxima Centauri is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">nearest star</a> to the Sun.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinematics">Kinematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Kinematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png/220px-PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png/330px-PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png/440px-PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18003-NASA-WISE-StarsNearSun-20140425-2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stars <a href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars_and_brown_dwarfs" title="List of nearest stars and brown dwarfs">closest</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, including <i>Alpha Centauri</i> (25 April 2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140425a_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140425a-65">[62]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>All components of Alpha Centauri display significant <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motion</a> against the background sky. Over centuries, this causes their apparent positions to slowly change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> Proper motion was unknown to ancient astronomers. Most assumed that the stars are immortal and permanently fixed on the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">celestial sphere</a>, as stated in the works of the philosopher Aristotle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> In 1718, <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a> found that some stars had significantly moved from their ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrometric" title="Astrometric">astrometric</a> positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berry_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-berry-68">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1830s, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henderson_(astronomer)" title="Thomas Henderson (astronomer)">Thomas Henderson</a> discovered the true distance to Alpha Centauri by analysing his many astrometric mural circle observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henderson1839_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson1839-69">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> He then realised this system also likely had a high proper motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitken_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Aitken-32">[31]</a></sup> In this case, the apparent stellar motion was found using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicolas_Louis_de_Lacaille" title="Nicolas Louis de Lacaille">Nicolas Louis de Lacaille</a>\'s astrometric observations of 1751&#8211;1752,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> by the observed differences between the two measured positions in different epochs.\n</p><p>Calculated proper motion of the centre of mass for Alpha Centauri AB is about 3620 mas (milli-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a>) per year toward the west and 694 mas/y toward the north, giving an overall motion of 3686 mas/y in a direction 11&#176; north of west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kervella2016_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kervella2016-74">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[note 4]</a></sup> The motion of the centre of mass is about 6.1&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcmin" title="Arcmin">arcmin</a> each century, or 1.02<a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">&#176;</a> each <a href="/wiki/Millennium" title="Millennium">millennium</a>. The velocity in the western direction is 23.0&#160;km/s and in the northerly direction 4.4&#160;km/s. Using <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> the mean radial velocity has been determined to be around 22.4&#160;km/s towards the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kervella2016_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kervella2016-74">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since Alpha Centauri AB is almost exactly in the plane of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> as viewed from Earth, there are many stars behind them. In early May 2028, Alpha Centauri A will pass between us and a distant red star, when there will be a 45% probability that an <a href="/wiki/Einstein_ring" title="Einstein ring">Einstein ring</a> will be observed. Other <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy)" title="Conjunction (astronomy)">conjunctions</a> will also occur in the coming decades, allowing accurate measurement of proper motions and possibly giving information on planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kervella2016_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kervella2016-74">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predicted_future_changes">Predicted future changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Predicted future changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Near-stars-past-future-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="999" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Near-stars-past-future-en.svg/330px-Near-stars-past-future-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Near-stars-past-future-en.svg/495px-Near-stars-past-future-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Near-stars-past-future-en.svg/660px-Near-stars-past-future-en.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Near-stars-past-future-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distances of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">nearest stars</a> from 20,000 years ago until 80,000 years in the future</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Cen_proper_motions.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="895" data-file-width="895" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Alpha_Cen_proper_motions.gif/280px-Alpha_Cen_proper_motions.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Alpha_Cen_proper_motions.gif/420px-Alpha_Cen_proper_motions.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Alpha_Cen_proper_motions.gif/560px-Alpha_Cen_proper_motions.gif 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Cen_proper_motions.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation showing motion of Alpha Centauri through the sky. (The other stars are held fixed for didactic reasons.) "Oggi" means today.</div></div></div>\n<p>As the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stars" title="Stars">stars</a> of Alpha Centauri move closer to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, their measured <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">proper motions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stellar_parallax" title="Stellar parallax">trigonometric parallaxes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial_velocities" title="Radial velocities">radial velocities</a> slowly increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matt93_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Matt93-33">[32]</a></sup>\nThese effects will continue until the star system reaches its nearest point to the Sun, and then reverse as the distance increases again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoubleStarsHeintz_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleStarsHeintz-29">[28]</a></sup> Furthermore, other small changes also occur with the binary star\'s <a href="/wiki/Orbital_elements" title="Orbital elements">orbital elements</a>. For example, in the apparent size of the semi-major axis of the orbital <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> will increase by 0.03&#160;arcsec per century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DoubleStarsHeintz_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-DoubleStarsHeintz-29">[28]</a></sup> Also the observed <a href="/wiki/Position_angle" title="Position angle">position angles</a> of the stars are also subject to small cumulative changes (additional to position angle changes caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precession_of_the_Equinoxes" title="Precession of the Equinoxes">precession of the equinoxes</a>), as first determined by W. H. van den Bos in 1926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[note 5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg/280px-Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg/420px-Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Motion-of-Alpha-Cen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apparent motion of Alpha Centauri relative to <a href="/wiki/Beta_Centauri" title="Beta Centauri">&#946; Centauri</a>. The minimum separation will actually be greater than shown in this figure.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Based on the system\'s common known proper motion and radial velocities, Alpha Centauri will continue to change its position in the sky significantly and will gradually brighten. For example, in about 6,200 AD, &#945; Centauri\'s true motion will cause an extremely rare <a href="/wiki/First_magnitude_star" title="First magnitude star">first-magnitude</a> stellar <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy)" title="Conjunction (astronomy)">conjunction</a> with <a href="/wiki/Beta_Centauri" title="Beta Centauri">&#946; Centauri</a>, forming a brilliant optical <a href="/wiki/Double_star" title="Double star">double star</a> in the southern sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOST2_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOST2-79">[74]</a></sup> It will then pass just north of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Cross" title="Southern Cross">Southern Cross</a> or <a href="/wiki/Crux" title="Crux">Crux</a>, before moving northwest and up towards the present <a href="/wiki/Celestial_equator" title="Celestial equator">celestial equator</a> and away from the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a>. By about 29,700&#160;AD, in the present-day constellation of <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(constellation)" title="Hydra (constellation)">Hydra</a>, Alpha Centauri will be 1.00&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">pc</a> or 3.3&#160;<a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">ly</a> away,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-80">[75]</a></sup> though later calculations suggest 0.90&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">pc</a> or 2.9&#160;<a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">ly</a> in 29,000&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AD" title="AD">AD</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailer2015_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailer2015-81">[76]</a></sup> At nearest approach, Alpha Centauri will attain a maximum <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of &#8722;0.86, comparable to present-day magnitude of <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>, but it will still not surpass that of <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a>, which will brighten incrementally over the next 60,000 years, and will continue to be the brightest star as seen from Earth (other than the Sun) for the next 210,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>About 28,000&#160;years from now, the Alpha Centauri system will begin to slowly move away from the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-80">[75]</a></sup> and this bright yellow star will eventually fall below naked-eye visibility.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_system">Planetary system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Planetary system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Confirmed_planets">Confirmed planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Confirmed planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">Proxima Centauri b</a></div>\n<p>Only one <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> has been confirmed for the Alpha Centauri system: Proxima Centauri b. It is slightly larger than the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and orbits around Proxima Centauri in the <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone" title="Circumstellar habitable zone">habitable zone</a>.  The existence of Proxima Centauri b was announced in 2016 by the European Southern Observatory.  It was found using the radial velocity method, where periodic Doppler shifts of spectral lines of the host star suggest an orbiting object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proxima_b_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-proxima_b-83">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversial_and_hypothetical_planets">Controversial and hypothetical planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Controversial and hypothetical planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_Bb">Alpha Centauri Bb<span id="Alpha_Centauri_Bc"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri Bb">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri_Bb" title="Alpha Centauri Bb">Alpha Centauri Bb</a></div>\n<p>In 2012, a planet around Alpha Centauri B was announced, <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri_Bb" title="Alpha Centauri Bb">Alpha Centauri Bb</a>, but in 2015 a new analysis concluded that it almost certainly does not exist and was just a spurious artefact of the data analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Centauri_Bc">Alpha Centauri Bc</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Alpha Centauri Bc">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The existence of a planet, Alpha Centauri Bc, was announced in 2013. It has an estimated <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a> of approximately 12 Earth days&#160;&#8211; less than that of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>&#160;&#8211; with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semimajor_axis" title="Semimajor axis">semimajor axis</a> of 0.10&#160;AU and an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> smaller than 0.24.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demory2015_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demory2015-87">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2015, transit results for Alpha Centauri B obtained using the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> were published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[83]</a></sup> They evidence a transit event possibly corresponding to a planetary body with a radius around 0.92&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth_radius" title="Earth radius"><var>R</var><sub>&#8853;</sub></a>. This planet would most likely orbit Alpha Centauri B with an orbital period of 20.4 days or less, with only a 5 percent chance of it having a longer orbit. The median of the likely orbits is 12.4 days with an impact parameter of around 0&#8211;0.3. Its orbit would likely have an eccentricity of 0.24 or less.  Like the probably spurious Alpha Centauri Bb, it likely has lakes of molten lava and would be far too close to Alpha Centauri B to harbour <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypothetical_planets">Hypothetical planets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hypothetical planets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Additional planets may exist in the Alpha Centauri system, either orbiting Alpha Centauri&#160;A or Alpha Centauri&#160;B individually, or in large orbits around Alpha Centauri AB. Because both stars are fairly similar to the Sun (for example, in age and <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>), astronomers have been especially interested in making detailed searches for planets in the Alpha Centauri system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2018)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> Several established planet-hunting teams have used various <a href="/wiki/Radial_velocity" title="Radial velocity">radial velocity</a> or star <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" title="Astronomical transit">transit</a> methods in their searches around these two bright stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-universetoday.com_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-universetoday.com-90">[85]</a></sup> All the observational studies have so far failed to find evidence for <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-universetoday.com_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-universetoday.com-90">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2009, computer simulations showed that a planet might have been able to form near the inner edge of Alpha Centauri B\'s habitable zone, which extends from 0.5 to 0.9 AU from the star. Certain special assumptions, such as considering that the Alpha Centauri pair may have initially formed with a wider separation and later moved closer to each other (as might be possible if they formed in a dense <a href="/wiki/Open_cluster" title="Open cluster">star cluster</a>), would permit an accretion-friendly environment farther from the star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thebault_2009_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thebault_2009-92">[87]</a></sup> Bodies around Alpha Centauri A would be able to orbit at slightly farther distances due to its stronger gravity. In addition, the lack of any brown dwarfs or gas giants in close orbits around Alpha Centauri make the likelihood of terrestrial planets greater than otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lackofany_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-lackofany-93">[88]</a></sup> A theoretical study indicates that a radial velocity analysis might detect a hypothetical planet of 1.8&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#8853;</sub></a> in Alpha Centauri B\'s <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone" title="Circumstellar habitable zone">habitable zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guedesetal2008_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-guedesetal2008-94">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Radial velocity measurements of Alpha Centauri B with <a href="/wiki/High_Accuracy_Radial_Velocity_Planet_Searcher" title="High Accuracy Radial Velocity Planet Searcher">High Accuracy Radial Velocity Planet Searcher</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectrograph" title="Spectrograph">spectrograph</a> ruled out planets of more than 4&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#8853;</sub> to<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the distance of the habitable zone of the star (orbital period P = 200 days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumusque_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dumusque-95">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Current estimates place the probability of finding an Earth-like planet around Alpha Centauri at roughly 85%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miniature_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-miniature-96">[91]</a></sup> The observational thresholds for planet detection in the habitable zones by the radial velocity method are currently (2017) estimated to be about 50&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#8853;</sub> for Alpha Centauri A, 8&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#8853;</sub> for Alpha Centauri B, and 0.5&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#8853;</sub> for <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao2018_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao2018-97">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early computer-generated models of planetary formation predicted the existence of <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a> around <a href="/wiki/Circumbinary_planet" title="Circumbinary planet">both Alpha Centauri A and&#160;B</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guedesetal2008_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-guedesetal2008-94">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[note 6]</a></sup> but most recent numerical investigations have shown that the gravitational pull of the companion star renders the accretion of planets difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thebault_2009_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thebault_2009-92">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> Despite these difficulties, given the similarities to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_type" title="Spectral type">spectral types</a>, star type, age and probable stability of the orbits, it has been suggested that this stellar system could hold one of the best possibilities for harbouring <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> on a potential planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiegert_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wiegert-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lackofany_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-lackofany-93">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> were probably crucial in perturbing <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> into the inner Solar System, providing the inner planets with a source of water and various other ices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell-102">[96]</a></sup> In the Alpha Centauri system, Proxima Centauri may have influenced the planetary disk as the Alpha Centauri system was forming, enriching the area around Alpha Centauri with volatile materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> This would be discounted if, for example, Alpha Centauri&#160;B happened to have gas giants orbiting Alpha Centauri&#160;A (or conversely, Alpha Centauri&#160;A for Alpha Centauri&#160;B), or if Alpha Centauri A and B themselves were able to perturb comets into each other\'s inner system as Jupiter and Saturn presumably have done in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell-102">[96]</a></sup> Such icy bodies probably also reside in <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort clouds</a> of other planetary systems.  When they are influenced gravitationally by either the gas giants or disruptions by passing nearby stars, many of these icy bodies then travel starwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell-102">[96]</a></sup> Such ideas also apply to the close approach of Alpha Centauri or other stars to the Solar System, when, in the distant future, the Oort Cloud might be disrupted enough to increase the number of active comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-80">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To be in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>, a planet around Alpha Centauri A would have an orbital radius of about 1.25&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>&#160;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span about="" title="habitable zones are supposed to be a range rather than a specific distance, is 1.25 AU just the distance where the energy from the Sun would be identical to Earth? Using the habitable zone calculator at depts.washington.edu/naivpl/content/hz-calculator with the effective temperature and luminosity of Alpha Centauri A given at springerreference.com/docs/html/chapterdbid/438742.html, the">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> so as to have similar planetary temperatures and conditions for liquid water to exist. For the slightly less luminous and cooler Alpha Centauri B, the habitable zone is closer at about 0.7&#160;AU (100&#160;million&#160;km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croswell_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-Croswell-102">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the goal of finding evidence of such planets, both Proxima Centauri and Alpha Centauri&#160;AB were among the listed "Tier&#160;1" target stars for <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Space_Interferometry_Mission" title="Space Interferometry Mission">Space Interferometry Mission</a> (SIM). Detecting planets as small as three Earth-masses or smaller within two <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> of a "Tier&#160;1" target would have been possible with this new instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-numbers_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-numbers-105">[99]</a></sup> The SIM mission, however, was cancelled due to financial issues in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circumstellar_discs">Circumstellar discs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Circumstellar discs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Based on observations between 2007 and 2012, a study found a slight excess of emissions in the 24&#160;&#181;m (mid/far-infrared) band surrounding <span class="nowrap">&#945; Centauri AB</span>, which may be interpreted as evidence for a sparse <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_disc" title="Circumstellar disc">circumstellar disc</a> or dense <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust_cloud" title="Interplanetary dust cloud">interplanetary dust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiegart2014_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiegart2014-107">[101]</a></sup> The total mass was estimated to be between 10<sup>&#8722;<span style="white-space:nowrap">7</span></sup> to 10<sup>&#8722;<span style="white-space:nowrap">6</span></sup> the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, or 10&#8211;100 times the mass of the Solar System\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zodiacal_cloud" title="Zodiacal cloud">zodiacal cloud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiegart2014_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wiegart2014-107">[101]</a></sup> If such a disc existed around both stars, <span class="nowrap">&#945; Centauri A\'s</span> disc would likely be stable to 2.8 AU, and <span class="nowrap">&#945; Centauri B\'s</span> would likely be stable to 2.5 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiegart2014_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wiegart2014-107">[101]</a></sup> This would put A\'s disc entirely within the <a href="/wiki/Frost_line_(astrophysics)" title="Frost line (astrophysics)">frost line</a>, and a small part of B\'s outer disc just outside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiegart2014_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wiegart2014-107">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="View_from_this_system">View from this system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Alpha_Centauri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: View from this system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="621" data-file-width="874" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6d/Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg/280px-Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6d/Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg/420px-Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6d/Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg/560px-Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sky-from-alpha-centauri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Looking towards the sky around <a href="/wiki/Orion_(constellation)" title="Orion (constellation)">Orion</a> from Alpha Centauri
